Term Project¶

Joshua Tran, 1001296598, CSE-5334

Objective:¶

The objective of this project is to produce a classifier that can take a look at a given review and produce a predicted rating. These reviews will be based off the datasets defined in the references for the 15 million reviews. Based off these criteria, I'll be implementing a NaiveBayes classifier along side with some pre-built sentiment analyzers to predict the rating of these reviews. However to accomplish this, a few things need to be noted about what has been done.

pre-processing the data:¶

The data is vast and large, some of the data needs to pre-processed to ease training, and to get rid of non-helpful information. From the given set of reviews, to simplifiy processing we've made all the classifications into integers 0-10 for 11 total classifications. keeping the floating point representations left review classification values in the thousands of total classifications and seemed over complicated and undesirable. So this has been cleaned up.
Additionally, I rid the data set from reviews that have no comment, because no comments doesn't help us predict actual comments.

Taking a look at sentiment analysis: "Vader" Sentiment¶

Now that we've taken a look at the data. Lets take a closer look at some sentiment analyzers. First we have "Vader" sentiment. This will take a given string and find the polarity of how positive, negative, and neutrual it is. Here we initiallize our analyzer, and give the first comment in our data to it and look at the scores.

In [7]:
analyzer = SentimentIntensityAnalyzer()
print(df.iloc[1]['comment'])
sentiment_polarity_score_example = analyzer.polarity_scores(df.iloc[1]['comment'])
print("result of sentiment polarity scores:\n",sentiment_polarity_score_example)

I tend to either love or easily tire of co-op games.  Pandemic joins Knizia's LoTR as my favorite true co-op.  It edges LoTR out merely in time to set-up and play.  LoTR can be an undertaking to explain enough details so that players enjoy their first time through the game, while Pandemic is fast enough that even if the players don't quite get everything that is going on, they can try again immediately.
result of sentiment polarity scores:
 {'neg': 0.0, 'neu': 0.786, 'pos': 0.214, 'compound': 0.9536}

Applying sentiment analysis to our data set.¶

Below we have a method to be used to apply our vader sentiment analyzer to the data set. The idea here is to use the values generated by our analyzer to produce numerical values to use that will weight a Naive Bayes classifier to produce predictions on what the actual weight of the sentiment is.
It is important to note that this processing takes a little bit of time, so i've generated a routine to check if the the processed sentiment has already been done, and if it has then we can just pull that data.

In [8]:
def features(x):
    result = analyzer.polarity_scores(x)
    return pd.Series([result['neg'], result['neu'], result['pos'], result['compound']])
In [9]:
polarity_score_path = 'C:\\Users\\j0sh7\\OneDrive\\Desktop\\Fall 2020\\Data mining\\term proj\\vader-processed.csv'
if(path.exists(polarity_score_path)):
    vader_processed_df = pd.read_csv(polarity_score_path)
# exporting data if it does not exists
else:
    df[['neg polarity','neu polarity','pos polarity','compound polarity']] = df['comment'].apply(features)
    df.to_csv(polarity_score_path,index=False)
    vader_processed_df = df

Here is a helper sub-routine to generate our accuracy of datasets.

In [12]:
def findAccuracy(actual, predictions):
    return ( np.sum(actual == predictions)/len(actual) )

Splitting the data¶

Now that we've applied our sentiment analysis to generate some features to determine a classification given a review. We need to seperate our data into a Training set and a Testing set. Below we generate a training set of 80% of the reviews and 20% of the reviews.

In [13]:
X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(np.stack(vader_processed_df[['neg polarity','neu polarity','pos polarity']].to_numpy()),vader_processed_df['rating'].values, test_size=0.2)

Using the Naive Bayes classifier¶

Here we use our Naive bayes classifier, along with a variety of different smoothing paramters to adjust the alpha value (lap-lace smoothing value) four our classifier. However as the data shows below, the hyper parameter applied has no effect on this dataset. and seems to maintain it's accuracy.

In [14]:
smoothingHyperParameter = [10.0, 5.0, 2.0, 1.0, 0.1, 0.01, 0.001, 0.0001, 0.00001]
for hyperParam in smoothingHyperParameter:
    MultinomialNB_clf = MultinomialNB(alpha=hyperParam)
    MultinomialNB_clf.fit(X_train, y_train)
    result = MultinomialNB_clf.predict(X_test)
    print(f"result of hyper parameter: {hyperParam} = {findAccuracy(y_test, result)}")

result of hyper parameter: 10.0 = 0.25147536331082376
result of hyper parameter: 5.0 = 0.25147536331082376
result of hyper parameter: 2.0 = 0.25147536331082376
result of hyper parameter: 1.0 = 0.25147536331082376
result of hyper parameter: 0.1 = 0.25147536331082376
result of hyper parameter: 0.01 = 0.25147536331082376
result of hyper parameter: 0.001 = 0.25147536331082376
result of hyper parameter: 0.0001 = 0.25147536331082376
result of hyper parameter: 1e-05 = 0.25147536331082376

Using TextBlob sentiment analyzer and Custom Classifier¶

Next lets try some new stuff. Here we try to do something similar to our previous approach. However, lets try to use TextBlob instead. Interestingly enough, TextBlob actually gives us the ability to use a custom classifer to predict what our sentiment is. Rather than just exclusively looking at positive and negative values.
HOWEVER, this training and classification process is EXTREMELY SLOW. So just to take a look at this, we'll be minimizing the data to around 10 thousand data points that will be split similarly to how we did previously in distribution for test and training sets.
The way this analyzer works is that it uses a feature extractor to send our given sets in and then apply a classifier(NaiveBayes) on it. However, for it to start producing prediction values it needs an initial comment/classification.
Additionally it uses an nltk classifier and wraps around it, making it slower than the sci-kit classifiers.

In [18]:
Xdrop, X, yDrop, y = train_test_split(df[['comment','rating']],df['rating'], test_size=(10000 / df.shape[0]))
X_train_TB, X_test_TB, y_train_TB, y_test_TB = train_test_split(X, y, test_size=.2,random_state=42)

 ("A rare beast. It is, at first blush, simply a beast. Its alarmingly long rule-set is filled with finicky exceptions. Its tabletop footprint is gigantic. It presents an intimidating menu of choices at every turn. And plan on setting aside 4-6 hours to play the solitaire game at a leisurely pace.  Yet though the barrier to entry is high, the riches to be found are greater still. Mage Knight performs the most dazzling feat of balance of any game I know. The countless possibilities are calibrated with extraordinary delicacy. As a result, despite the game's complexity and its many sources of chance - card shuffles, tile draws, dice rolls - the game is almost purely one of skill. But somehow none of this comes at the expense of thematic immersion. All of the moving parts in this dense strategic masterpiece make good thematic sense, leaving you with the feeling of a powerful Mage Knight exploring the land and conquering ever more terrifying foes.  Mage Knight is the tabletop equivalent of War and Peace: grand, challenging, and immensely satisfying.", 10.),

 ("Bus is an interesting splotter game, and for me personally I think one of the better ones. It's not nearly so obtuse/opaque as many of the others, allowing for players to more easily grasp what is going on. Yet there are still delightfully difficult decisions, players can and will absolutely be devastated by others decisions. The way worker placement works here creates insane amounts of tension in fighting for actions and positioning yourself. The fact each player only has 20 total actions but can choose to use a bunch in one round is also a very clever touch.   It plays in that 60-90 time limit I'm more of a fan of as well for most games. Like most splotter titles, a few bad plays by any one player can really hose them and throw the game to someone else. For that reason I really would only want to play this with a group that dedicates to playing it a few times to get more experience. Else I think the experience is significantly weakened. Given I'm not really in that type of group right now, I'm less interested in really getting this repeated plays--and even if I did find a group like that I think there are other more interesting games to study deeper.  ('My favorite designer has put together another winner. While the high fantasy theme of Mage Knight is a fairly  dull, run-of-the-mill, orc-fest -- the game itself is epic.  Most of the stuff we see in this genre is either a dungeon crawl (like decent), or some sort of Magic: The Gathering clone.  Mage Knight tells a much broader tale.  Instead of trudging through a dungeon or engaging in some sort of spell-slinging wizard duel, you trudge through multiple dungeons and sling many spells.  You also raise your own army or followers and can even burn down the occasional monastery just for fun.  There are several Vlaada trademarks in this design.  One of which is his tendency to resolve critical gameplay moments with a kind of puzzle solving rather than a die role.  The plus side is that it can be very satisfying to play your hand of cards in just the right way as to move to the wizard tower and defeat the guardian there, while still having enough left over to bank a mana crystal for the next round.  The downside is that sometimes there are moments in the game that lose their dramatic punch as everything has already been worked out by the time it\'s your turn.  It\'s one thing to see a battle hinge on that critical die roll it\'s another to have the maths already in your pocket and have it all just "work out".  Still there is so much variety and strategic opportunities in this game that it really deserves your attention.  It\'s a true exploration game done right(much like High Frontier actually), where you really feel like you are earning every new encounter, every spell, every artifact.  Warning:  This is best explored first on your own (a few solo rounds will be really helpful), and then probably with one other experienced player.  Don\'t dive into a 4-player game with all newbies.', 8.),

It is supposed to be a family game but most people find it too "complicated" for casual play.  It never becomes as popular even as pseudo-wargame like "Risk" who has a subject on war, a subject supposedly not appeal to the general public as much as a detective case on murder.  You ask questions and the one has the answer next to you passes the information ( a la card) he has on you.  ("I don't enjoy playing this game. At the risk of repeating all of the other criticisms out there, here are my thoughts...  The biggest problem is the rules don't feel like give the players any choices or control at all -- you either explore a new tile and trigger a monster  and possible an encounter, or you trigger an encounter (even if you're actively engaged in combat.) The monsters and encounters often include unavoidable damage. Most powers become exhausted after a single use and don't reset until you  manage to get one of the very few dedicated cards that reset them (even resetting on a natural 20 would feel more balanced and exciting.) This means that you end up choosing between your at-will powers for almost the entire game. And to make it worse, most of the powers don't have enough of an effect to merit their use. For example, one power allows you to push back a monster, but this doesn't in any way limit the monster's ability to attack you when it activates in almost all cases. So you might have well just used the at will power with the highest bonus to hit.  Speaking of bonuses, the monsters power seems quite arbitrary. Kobold skirmishers have +9, which means they hit the most heavily armored character on a 6 or something close to that. Really strange.  Any game I've played without the Cleric feels like punishment as opposed to a game. The items are unremarkable and often come with rules or limitations that make them feel weak.  The characters and their abilities are ripped right from the pages of D&D source material, so that shouldn't disappoint.  The quality of the components varies. For instance, the encounter, treasure, and item cards are terribly bland with no art. The monster cards have some art and are ok. The highlight being the interlocking room tiles and the miniatures which are nice enough to justify the purchase if you can get it for under forty bones, which shouldn't be too difficult.", 4.),

 ("I didn't know what to expect going in, but being as that I tend to usually like roll-and-writes, I wanted to check this out.  ('Fast passed word game where you string all your letters together to create a crossword looking thing. Really fun word game. Better than Scrabble but in the same vein. ', 8.),
 ("I preordered this game (and received some nifty resin bonus miniatures and terrain as a reward) and got it to the table not too soon after it arrived. We played two of the scenarios and while a few mistakes were made the first time around the second play went smoothly.  The game itself is a co-op with each player taking the role of one or more of the crew of the Firefly. There are only five roles available in the base game, though the word is that more will follow in the upcoming expansions.  The game pits the crew against a combination of goons (either thugs who are all about hand-to-hand combat or Cowboys who 'pack heat' and will shoot when able) and uses a activation system based on 'moments'. That is, each mission (referred to, in the spirit of the game as a 'job') runs for a certain number of moments before the job is deemed a bust.  Players use differing number of moments for their actions and then the player (or goon) furthest back on the moment track is activated. This works well but in a four player (and, I imagine a five player) game it can lead to a fair bit of downtime depending on where players are placed.  One nice touch is that players have the option of either acting casual or acting heroic. This determines not only what actions are available to them but also the way the goons will interact with them as well.  The game is good clean silly fun and has excellent production qualities.   A couple of weaknesses are that there are no 'boss' style opponents and fighting the goons, even though there are five varieties of each, can get old fast. The other main weakness is that there are only four jobs included in the pack. I'd expect a dozen to be the bare minimum but Gale Force Nine have advised that plenty more will be made available on-line soon.  Overall, a good fun co-op which does an excellent job in capturing the theme of this much-loved TV show. The Little Fish (aged nearly 8 and 5 and a half) though seemed to really like this and I can see it hitting the table again soon.  A winner.", 8.),

weight action selection (draft a ressource cube) / worker placement (invest a worker into a profession) game with some paths to victory. ++ The fact of having multiple generation of workers, that die, and to score this, is really innovative. ++ Using the notion of passing time as an alternative ressources to do stuff is really innovative as well and really well implemented in the game theme as passing time eventually kills your workers, in order of generation (oldest workers die first). ++ I really like that all the gameplay information is well blended on the board (action information are depicted clearly on the roof of buildings). ++ There are expansions available for this game (which is useful to refresh the game when we have overplayed it).   :thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]  -- Although we should like everything about this game, it doesn't seem to have clicked as much as other game in this kind, probably due to the lack of an engine-building aspect (you're not building up your family, you just play them until they die). -- The replayability seem to be limited without any expansions, as there seem to be only a few viable strategy to follow (at least in a 2-player game). -- The colored cubes, although typical in a Euro and practical because we have to draw them blind for the setup, doesn't easily evoke the idea behind them (skill, faith, knowledge, etc.) and thus player just refer to them as brown cube, instead of Faith cube for example. It would have been awesome that they could have use some wooden disks with silkscreen on it with images that could have convey the theme better (but I know this would have increase the costs). -- The printing on the rule book and the board is a little bit sub par (the surface is rough instead of being smooth). -- I hate having to put sticker on game parts (but I know why they do it instead of printing on the parts).   :arrowE: [b]OTHER GAME INFO:[/b] [b] Game Weight = 3.1/5 Release Date = 2011 Number of Player = 2-4 2-player length = 60 min.  ('2020-06-24  Initial Rating: 7.0 (June 2020)  I love Summoner Wars and last month I played Ashes: Rise of the Phoenixborn and I liked it as well, so I was very keen to try another 2 player game from Plaid Hat Games and (in this case) designer Colby Dauch. Sadly, even though the game began very strong for me, at the end I was kind of bored.  Crystal Clans rules are easy to teach. You can do it under 7 minutes. Playtime is around 100 minutes which is too long.  The theme is kind of nice, but the story in the rulebook is rather weak. The theme is very thin and you dont feel it when you play the game, but I dont care about theme if the game has good mechanisms (Crystal Clans doesnt).  The art is merely ok, I dont really like it (Summoner Wars art is also regular but I love the game) while I do love the art in Ashes: Rise of the Phoenixborn.  Just like the other games by from Plaid Hat Games, there are lots of options to choose from before you begin playing and the decks came already assembled (which is great). Just like Summoner Wars, each deck has 3 champions (called Heroes here) and the rest are common units (there are no events). I like this.   Gameplay is fun at the beginning. To do any action you have to first pay the cost and each time you pay the cost, you give that amount of energy to your opponent, so if you play cheap cards, your opponent will have few energy in his turn, if you play big cards, your opponent will have enough energy to play big cards as well, this also means that if you have lots of energy, you can play several times in a row until you run out of energy.  I LOVE this, this is the best facet of the game.  Each player can summon units to the board, but you can have at most 3 units in the same space. There is a stacking mechanism which sounds great on paper, but it is a hassle in real life as you have to constantly reorganize the cards each time you move them. Also there is a faction that can ignore this rule (obviously), the Blood Clan, and they can have stack much more bigger than 3, so this part is rather boring having to reorganize constantly the stacks during the game. After you have units on the board you can move them and you can attack with them. The combat system is very boring, you simply sum the amount of attack you have in your stack and then you compare it with the defense of the opponents units. You kill the units which have less shields than your attack from top to bottom, your opponent does the same and the wounds are dealt at the same time. Players also play a facedown card to affect the combat, but this part revolves around a boring rock-paper-scissor scheme and the end result is random. I VASTLY prefer to roll dice In which the odds are clearly and easily calculated, in Crystal Cavern the hidden cards can have a HUGE impact in the battle or they can do nothing and it is worse if you dont have cards in hand as in that case you play the first card from your deck and you get a random effect (as you never saw it). I dont like uncontrollable luck in games, but I do love to throw dice in cmoabt games (and the odds are easily calculable so it isnt really uncontrollable), and I miss them here.  The goal of the game is not to kill your opponent and I also think I dont like this. The goal here is to be the first to have 4 Crystals and you get them (mainly) by controlling 2 out of 3 key spaces on the board (the board size is 9, so it is very small). Once you do so, you can buy a crystal out of 3. All the crystals have an effect and they appear randomly (I dislike this). Some crystal are clearly more powerful than others, other are much more situational so there is a lot of uncontrollable luck here. The other way to acquire a Crystal is to deplete your opponents deck and trigger a shuffle to draw a new card.  We played with the Blood Clan, which has weak units, but they can stack in numbers greater than 3, still I found them to be rather weak. They die very easily and you need much more skill to play them than I anticipated. We also played with Meteor Clan which has VERY strong units, albeit they are very costly, but 3 of these units are more than a match that 8 of the Blood Clan Units. Just like Summoner Wars, I guess the different factions have different style of play so I wont accuse the game of being unbalanced but I felt the Blood Clan has a much harder time winning than the Meteor Clan.  I think there is a deckbuilding option, but those rules werent explained to me. I do like the option exists.  Bottom line, I was expecting great things from Crystal Clans, but I found a mediocre game. Combat is boring (the main culprit), without tension and random with the rock-paper-scissor mechanism. I also dislike the fact that your goal is not to kill your opponent (this is very personal though, not a minus against the game). I miss throwing dice in tactical 2 player games, who would have guessed? The energy system (Im not sure it is called this way) is brilliant and the best the game has to offer. Perhaps with more plays the game could grow on me and I have a small curiosity about the other factions, but my desire to play the game remains low so I will sell it ASAP. I could probably play it in the future in the opportunity arises.   Current Rating: 5.0', 5.),

 ("Used to be pretty big into Magic back in the 90's - early 2000's.  don't play it at all these days but still have thousands of cards in the closet.  ", 6.),
 ('Simple and fast. A very good intro or filler.  It\'s been compared to Splendor, however aside from the fact that there are public contracts (cards) that all players are vying for, the mechanics are not very similar.  First, the resource pool in Century is not limited. That means that your "plan" on what to do on your next turn can\'t be altered significantly by what other players do. This keeps the game moving at a brisk pace, sometimes comically fast.  Second, Century has a deck-building and engine building aspect. The spices that you gather, and your ability to upgrade spices, is directly impacted by the cards that you add to you caravan (hand). You thus have control of your own destiny in terms of how you build you spice gathering and trading empire. You have the ability to formulate a strategy and customize your caravan towards the cards that you want, and players may grow differently meaning that they\'re not necessarily in direct competition.  That makes the game not so much about who can get certain things first, but who can do things the best. That, to me, makes the game less confrontational and more enjoyable.', 7.),

 ('Smash up is not a deck-building game, per say.  At the beginning of the round, you combine two different decks and then fight it out with other players.  ("Now this is a city builder! Each round in this game players build 1-3 building tiles, or they build 1, and expand the area they are allowed to cover on their boards. The building tiles are tetris pieces, and their placement rules and abilities change slightly depending on their size. Similar to in Town Center, to have a larger building it needs a certain number of adjacent tiles of the correct colors. This can be extremely challenging, because while this is all happening you need to worry about creating enough jobs to attract new meeples to your board, and you need to worry about managing your pollution levels, because each turn you accrue pollution which converts directly to negative points at the end of the game, and worse yet, other players can send one of their meeples to your city, to get in your way and take those crucial action spaces on your buildings that you so carefully constructed.  The game is complicated, tense and really fantastic. I can't wait for more plays of this game.  :star: Art, Iconography and components functional :star: Clever or interesting mechanisms :nostar: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players :star: Multiple plays stay interesting :star: Low or mitigatable luck elements :star::star::star::star::nostar: Raw enjoyment", 8.),

 ('Crystal Clans does certainly feel like a Colby Dauch game, inspired by his classic Summoner Wars but nowhere near imitating it. At its basic point CC has asymmetric factions that are intriguing and change your style of game based on which faction you control. The game also seems inspired by a sort of "Team Fortress" theme where you have to run out into the common field and hold two regions for points, but making yourself open to attack. The initiative track is brilliant and might be worth the price of admission itself. The artwork looks very much like something you might find in an iPad game, so you might like that style or not. Melding armies together and selecting your special army power gives you something to think about as well. And controlling your draw pile size to prevent your opponent from getting a free crystal (1 of 4 points to win) adds another layer to the game. With all these great things being said, I still found the experience a little muddy and felt somewhat chaotic at times - not really a feeling I like. Something like SW gives more opportunity for plotting and building a plan and CC is mean and unforgiving right from the get-go - which I agree is a flavour of the game and not a fault. While combat is a big part of the game it is not an overwhelming part - but I\'m not sure I care that much for the combat. Maybe it\'s better than dice, but it felt a little sloggy adding up different attack and defense values, considering special powers, and then finally adding in the "battle values" in the end - which works like a rock/scissors/papers thing, but feels way more random than maybe it\'s supposed to. It adds a bit of "fog of war" to a game which might not be any better than chucking dice (though it might be more interesting). I would imagine repeated plays would improve the flow and familiarity with the factions would reveal more strategic options. So in the end: Crystal Clans is a very good game that didn\'t win my affections immediately but has the possibility of courting my favour has time goes on.', 7.),

 ("Stefan Feld revisits the head-to-head Roman combats, with wolves, bears, lions and triremes in the Colosseum... well, maybe it's not quite THAT thematic, but Arena: Roma II presents both a nice stand-alone game, with some nice enhancements to the orginal (e.g., the Bribery slot,) as well as what seems a more balanced set of cards for the duelers.  Like Roma this is a one-on-one, may the best player win game.  The same set of 3 dice are used to activate cards, and similar empty slot penalties are in play.   Instead of the 1-6 pip discs, Arena: Roma II provides a nice belt built of interlocking sections, and it adds a new slot to the six basic ones, which raises the number needing to be filled, but also the number of available cards. The new slot- the Bribery slot- is available with any die, but requires sesterces matching the pips to activate the card there. This means with funds it can be used very frequently, and this can create some interesting strategies all on it's own.  After a first solo play I give this a 7.5; making it among Feld's best games, and enhancing an older one with a couple of flaws.  Nothing like improving on a good idea! ", 8.),

 ("[b][i]Axis & Allies[/i][/b] was my (and many others') introduction to war games. Created in 1981, this is still the standard for global-scale World War 2 board games. However, gameplay wise the slow player-by-player turn sequence and simple (but still fun!) dice combat make A&A feel dated relative to more recent games. This game is nominally 5-player--and it's great if you can get a willing enthusiastic group. However, the long wait times between turns make this most easily played with 2 people.  Perhaps it's because I've had so many good times with this game, but I hope that everyone tries this game at least once. Unfortunately for my nostalgia, I think games such as Hannibal: Rome vs. Carthage, War of the Ring, A Game of Thrones: The Board Game, or Warrior Knights are better games. But I [i]would[/i] recommend A&A for people interested in learning the basic flow and geography of World War 2.  The modified map, inclusion of destroyers, and various other tweaks lead me to prefer the 2004 edition over previous editions.", 7.),

The thing is, that castles and towns, where you want to be first are adjacent to a lot of different types of fields (which does make it easier to connect to, but harder to connect from). - The mid-game is more interesting as you have more choice, unless, you're like me and have drawn many of the same types of fields in the previous turns.  ("A clone of DC Comics Deck-Building Game, but as odd as it sounds, I think the theme works better here. You'll be skating, passing, drawing penalties -- well, abstractly.  ('These games are a one off dinner/party game, they are quite good if everyone gets into the theme of things. a good laugh.', 8.),
 ('Obviously inspired by Sid Meier\'s Civilization (computer game), this conversion to a board/card game works much better than Eagle\'s earlier attempt. Much more abstract and without a map, this version still has players building and tailoring their "civilization" according to their inclinations and drafting cards that support the direction they wish to take (or sometimes the reverse).  As in so many games lately, the player is regulated by an "action drafting" system where each action involves making choices between many options. The game is essentially one of constructing a victory point engine from the opportunities the game presents.  The game consists of three levels: "simple," "advanced" and "full." The simple game involves very little player interaction and serves mainly to introduce all the moving parts. The advanced game adds more to the game and can involve more player interaction (a lot more if all the optional rules are adopted). The game requires several playings to appreciate the flow and card interactions. With a full four players, the advanced game becomes quite long - the full game would be epic (and since being simply more of the same, I\'m not sure the full game will ever need to be played except by fanatics). Three players may be the sweet spot (and I\'ve not tried two players).  The player interaction, when it occurs, consists of taking "military actions" such as playing an "aggression" card which represents a raid or skirmish between two players with the winner being the one with the higher total military strength. In this, the defender has a slight advantage being the only one able to play "defense cards" he may hold. Attacking may mean committing to lose some expensive military units, so doing so (unless almost certain of winning) seems to be a needless gamble. In practice, it means that a player will attack the weakest opponent rather than your nearest competitor and thus tends not to be a particularly useful tool (and a possible design flaw). Nothing would frustrate a player more than investing several hours to his civilization only to see it savaged by an opportunist. So far in our games we haven\'t seen a lot of this sort of "take that" play - but we can see the possibility of this becoming an important element.  Another related aspect of player interaction is the competition for colonies and new territories where lucrative resources are acquired by bidding military units against the other players. However, as successfully doing so could leave you vulnerable to aggression, it becomes a very calculated risk.  It occurs to me that most players drawn to this game will be those who see this as a Euro version of Civ. The cutthroat nature of the player interaction may prove to be a turn off for many of those. So far I\'m liking it (but have yet to see or feel the full force of the nasty things players can inflict on each other).  You have been warned - Caveat Emptor! ++++++ After an extended hiatus, I\'ve had a chance to revisit this game and must admit to the genius of its design. I still think three players is the sweet spot but my hesitance about the military side of the game is proving unfounded. It is integral and self-balancing, and will be visited a lot or a little depending on the group dynamic but the game will stand up either way. Truly impressive!', 10.),
 ('2020: 3 Spiele 2019: 8 Spiele 2018: 4 Spiele 2017: 9 Spiele', 8.),

 ("[COLOR=#663300][b][size=11]Blue Moon City[/size][/b][/COLOR] [COLOR=#663300][i]Note: My numeric ratings disregard BGG's associated verbal descriptions.[/i][/COLOR]  [COLOR=#006600][b]Comments: [/b][/COLOR] Addictive.  Neighborhood bonuses are perhaps the most interesting element, as the order in which they are revealed can entirely change the relative value of surrounding tiles.  This can be great when the revealed bonus is highly valuable and you have a presence on the surrounding tiles.  But if you don't have such a presence, swooping in and revealing a low value bonus [b]before[/b] a neighboring high value bonus is revealed can prevent your opponents from cleaning up.  This game is joining my favorites.  I find that I always enjoy it.  Some games warrant great ratings based on various attributes, but the actual [i]play[/i] may feel like work to me.  Not so with Blue Moon City.  Based on attributes such as its variable board, production quality, ease of play, and such, I think Blue Moon City does warrant relatively high ratings, but my own experience of the game goes beyond objective analysis -- I just find it really fun.  Hence, I've continued to revise my rating a bit higher after every play.  [COLOR=#006600][b]Why we own it: [/b][/COLOR]  If I have to name a deciding feature, it's the neighborhood bonuses, which remain once a tile is completed and flipped.  But of course the factor really responsible for our decision to give this game space in our crowded house is that we have fun playing it.  ("Despite a pleasing thematic gloss, Three Kingdoms is a eurogame with worker placement, production engine building, a touch of area control, and a veritable Chef's Salad of points, with nine things scoring at game end.  Battles there are at the borders between the three clans, but they boil down to bidding contests.    Three Kingdoms adds interest to dull old worker placement in two ways.  First, each worker is a general with three different stats.   Some actions demand combat ability, others administration, while going into battle calls for combat and leadership.  On top of that, every general has a different special ability.  An important part of the game is to exploit that ability as much as possible and to find effective ways of combining it with others and with enhancements.  Second, in the early game contesting an action or a border area does not seem critical - indeed, in Three Kingdoms' all or nothing world it risks being wasteful.  In the late game, with time running out and end game points clearly at stake, there is much more contesting and this begins to feel more like a bidding game.   Three Kingdoms also asks tricky questions about when to station a general on the border.  Area control of your two borders is an end game scorer, while military presence there scores points every turn.  On the other hand, stationing a general robs you of a worker and, quite likely, their special ability.  And you need to pay their troops with rice and gold.    The game's point salady nature does present strategic choices, since focusing on a few of the ways to score is essential.    For a first published design, Three Kingdoms is remarkably polished.  The one element that could have used more work is the enhancements that offer end game vps, often with a condition for building that is only likely to be met in the early game.  Drew one of those later on?  Hard luck.  The artwork is outstanding.  The only misstep was with the army counters, whose trained and untrained sides look too darned similar and evoke ducks more than soldiers.  Production quality of my Capstone edition is very good.  The rules are fine, with a good use of examples.", 7.),

In my experience, rarely is the person who is the most expert in trivia the one who wins this game.  That is fine by me.  Even the totally clueless can do well if they guess right (or manage to blindly follow the appropriate people).  All in all, I find this game to be fun to play as opposed to the mental masturbation that most trivia games are at heart.  [02/17/2008]", 8.),

 ("Became a staple of the group of 20 something I play with. It's simple, social and produces a lot of laughs - great as a session starter to set up the mood. While being compared to Coup, it's a different type of beast altogether. It's unique in hidden role games for not being about elimination, but accumulation of wealth/points (control of game's lenght) and because roles will constantly shift. It's not so much about lying as it is about [b]confusion distribution[/b]. The more confusion you sow and lets confused you are, better off you'll be. Cards flying around, being shuffled, remembered, forgotten, then a couple of players try to block those threatening to win.  The chaos definitely has a Faiduttean signature, but it's like you're in control of the volume button (and you want it turned to 11).  ('2012-06-13  Initial Rating: 8.5 (May 2012)  What a surprise! When I played Indonesia I didn`t expect to find this jewel of a game. If you like economic games this is a must! As with Antiquity, this game can be very unforgiving, if you do wrong choices at the beginning of the game you are, effectively, out of the game, which is a plus for me :D.   It seems it has different viable paths to victory but I have to play more to verify this.  The game is marvelous, however for an expensive game like this I found the errors on the game board to be unforgivable (there are 3 print errors on the map as well as on some cards). The Indonesia map is pretty but it doesnt help the gameplay in any way, also the rulebook is confusing at times (but the rules are relatively simple).  In conclusion, Indonesia is a great gamers game, fit for only hardcore gamers due to its heaviness and time factor. A must have for me.  ('Danger Circuit board, Civilized and Luck Promo Cards, are all included in the base box.', 8.),
 ("A wonderful tile placement game from Renier Knizia. The components are top notch, from the puzzle piece boards to the hex tiles and the plastic figurines. The only downsides are that the boards are a bit on the thin side and the '1', stylized to look like Sinic character writing like many things set in East Asia, looks like a '7', which can be confusing to new players.  The game play is very simple and surprisingly fast. Lay all the figurines on to the cities, with no two same types of figures on the same city, one at a time in turn order. Afterwards, just place a tile, take another turn if it has a kanji on it, then refresh to five tiles. If a city is completely surrounded by land tiles, the players with the most powerful tiles (indicated by the number on the tile) wins the figurine. As I said, it's very easy to explain and quick to play, although that is adjustable depending on how AP-tolerant players are (that a plus and minus, if the players agree on not).   The biggest problem is with end game victory conditions. It's not that hard, but it's still on the wonky side. It's bit like Lost Cities in that way. It's best when explaining the game, to put this first and foremost. Definitely worth getting.", 10.),

 ("This is a very unique game in that it combines a word game with a dexterity game and throws in a speed component.  Random letter tiles are placed in a plastic tray (face-down, unlike the photo) that has snake-like tracks. Dice are thrown to determine the length (3-7 letters) and type (noun or verb) of word to be created. Then, all players scramble to get tiles out of the tracks. You can grab from any track, but it has to be from the front. Additionally, if you want to return a letter, you must put it in at the back, which sometimes forces you to waste time sliding tiles in order to make room for it. As soon as someone has created a word that fits the criteria, and has no extra tiles in front of them, they ring the bell and end the round.  The first player scores a small bonus plus a point for every letter in the word. Remaining players can score a point per letter for *any* word (even if it doesn't fit the criteria) that they've completed, minus a point for any extra tiles. This scoring system makes it very interesting, since you should try to have at least some word to reduce the damage if you're not the first one done.  It was very chaotic, but that made it fun and it played very quickly. Vocabulary helped, of course, but it wasn't as essential as it is in other games.", 6.),
 ('A thematic game. It plays competitively and each faction plays differently. Factions not entirely balanced. VERY difficult and expensive to collect and buy this game. The figures are nice.  ('[b]THE DARK SIDE:[/b]  1. Dice-rolling luckfest. 2. Suck-it big-time collectability. 3. Bendy TIE fighters and X-Wings. 4. Simple ruleset. 5. Rubbish spacemap. 6. Horrendously expensive. 7. No scenarios. 8. Scale issues.  [b]THE LIGHT SIDE:[/b]  1. It\'s freakin\' Star Wars! In starships! 2. Peaow! Peaow! Zing! Zing! Wooosh! Kafizz! KABLOOOOOM! 3. You get to move the Millenium Falcon, and Slave-1 around your dining table with other adults, while making sound effects like those mentioned in No.2. 4. The miniatures are very good, detailed, nicely painted. 5. You can craft your own killer system of rules. 6. The artwork/graphic design on the ship stat cards is cool. 7. Some of the rules are kinda neat; the stat-card damage flip, and the ship class structure springs to mind. Actually, I quite dig the starfighter blocking mechanic as well. 8. Did I mention the whole Star Wars thing? In starships?   Okay, Okay. I hear the negative vibes, baby. I hear \'em. Loud and clear. BUT - and it\'s a big but - set this beauty up on a custom game-mat and invite people \'round. Nothing draws in a gaggle of geeks faster than a wing of TIEs overshadowed by Star Destroyers. Nothing. Not even close. This is the one you want. Gameplay is a standard, simple, no fuss system of to-hit shield/maneuver ratings, move x-number of squares per turn ships, and rolling a skillets of dice to represent banks of turbolasers blasting. It works. Quickly and efficiently. Having said that the gameplay is simple, it does include a few buffs that shine it up, such as the one-shot strategic command actions. You may also build your own fleets using a Warhammer-style points mechanism; with a few minor exceptions - Rebel Cruiser pops into my thoughts - the ships are evenly rated point-for-point. The game will allow Droid Starfighters matched against TIEs and other nonsense; though my personal taste is to keep separate the Galactic Civil War and the earlier Clone Wars. But that\'s just me. Nostalgic.   Like Zombies! just make up your own rules if you hate these. Or, better yet, slightly tweak the ones present - so as to still use the stat cards, etc. The pesky bent figures can be un-bent, so stop whining about that. A few also like their space battles played out on hex-maps, but I actually prefer the squares; it\'s simple, quick, and less fussy. Also, the model scale troubles some folks. I don\'t see why. If you want the Executor to the same scale, you\'d need it to be 20\' long and resting on a wheeled bed. Then you and your scale-freak buddies would need to push the bed around a small farmyard, dodging chickens; the TIEs resting on a nearby bedstand. It might rain...  [b]I rate this an "7" ONLY if I play using my own house rules.[/b] The game then has the chance to evolve into big, brash Star-Warsy fun.  [i]WARNING:[/i] If you want to play themed games, such as just ships from the Galactic Civil War, then be prepared to give Starship Battles a serious cash injection. You may have to remortgage your trailer.  [b]JEDI MASTER TIPS:[/b]  A. Limit the weapon ranges for Class 1,2, and 3 starships to 4 squares (on a 11/17 or 18x24 grid). This forces the bigger starships to actually, you know, manoeuvre about the play area.  B. Roll 2D10 rather than 1D20 to hit. C. Have Class 1 & 2 ships block LOS.  D. Create some scenarios. The simplest, and most effective, scenario is to have the matchup resemble War at Sea: * players agree on a Fleet points total. * place 3 objective tokens separately somewhere abouts the middle section of the map you are using. * gift each token with a victory point value (50-100 points). * agree a points-based winning condition (a total of ships atomised and objective tokens captured). [i]For the objective token to be claimed, the player must have a Class 1-3 starship in the square, and no enemy ships in any adjacent square.[/i]  E. Buy a custom space-mat. F. Choose your Fighter Launch ships wisely. G. Add "space terrain" like asteroid fields, planets, etc, to liven up the map.  H. Increase the effectiveness of the following ships: [b]Luke\'s X-wing, Vader\'s TIE advanced, Boba Fett\'s Slave One,[/b] and the [b]Millennium Falcon[/b] by firstly removing the attack bonus Class 1&2 starships receive with ALL of their weapons when they attack those unique vessels; secondly by having them only able to target them when they close within a 2-square range. Yes, that makes them point-for-point a better deal than, say, Virago but, quite frankly, I don\'t care and neither should you. This ruling increases the utility of those iconic ships, and stops them exploding like balloons at the start of every match - which is what usually happens, because no one wants the Falcon screaming around hosing down their TIE squadrons; so they target them with every turbolaser in the Galaxy. Every true Star Wars nut wants those particular starships zooming about the game until a few turns have passed, at least, and these rules gives them a bloody chance! Now, players will have to get their fighters moving, if they want to kill Boba Fett, who\'s been haranguing their transports. ', 7.),

 ("1 play  This is an action-selection set-collection sort of game, with a very-much pasted on theme. The action spaces are characters from the Holmes universe, each with a different effect. There are 3 standard actions always available, the rest being random (2 at start, another added each of the 6 rounds after the first). Players choose 3 different actions each round; any action chosen by both will be unavailable the following round. Actions allow players to gain search tokens (the currency of the game) or use them to acquire cards (either from a display, the deck, or maybe the other player). Cards are kept face up in personal displays, sorted by type; each card comes in a set of size 3-9. At the end, the player with the most of each card type scores the set value (=size of set) less the number of cards of that type which the other player collected. There are a few little twists (some cards face down and protected, optional cards to increase difficulty) but that's the gist, highest sum wins!  Decent, but not nearly as good as my favoirte 2er classic Battle Line!", 6.),

 ("Finally took this out of the box about 18 years after purchase (10 of which it spent in storage).  My interest had been rekindled bt the good reputation the game has, and the fact that it is being used in some newer games.  At this point, the evaluation is preliminary, but I'm a bit underwhelmed. There is a lot of good stuff in there (love the fog of war concepts, the notion of concentrated fire), and the game has an intriguing scale that makes division-sized battles quite manageable.  However, I'm less enamoured of the chitpull system here than I thought I'd be, even though I'm a dyed-in-the wool chitpull fan all the way back to Across 5 Aprils. There just doesn't seem to be much of a feeling of the OODA loop that the best tactical systems out there (such as Tank Leader or TCS) generate at platoon scale. Yet, one would assume that by going up in scale, those effects are regimental/division command level would be [i]more[/i] pronounced, not less.  So this may be a good game and better than the hopeless crop of 1970s games without any command control systems, but perhaps not quite as good as I thought. Will play more to figure this out.    Finally, as others have pointed out, that the Germans activate by regiments and so separate out infantry and tanks misses one of the key advantages of the German approach during the war.  ('Great set of rules, especially for the time.  ('A good starter game to get the girls involved. Pretty looking, simple to play and has some strategy but not a lot of depth', 7.),
 ("I've always enjoyed chess, but as a game I feel like it devolves once one side has been largely taken out and suddenly you have to figure out how to pin the King down. Maybe if you have memorized end game moves, you will know how to make this a smooth and quick process. But I've always found it very difficult to achieve, and very easy to continue evading a capture, dragging the game out artificially (with, sometimes, a surprise turnaround). Ultimately, it's a bit tedious.  (This sort of tedious, extended game of trying to pin down ferrets is also the impression I get from my few tries at Go, and possibly why I wasn't particularly attracted to it. At least Chess has a part which isn't that!)  From a greater standpoint, I feel like the game was always intended to be a great simulation of war, and allow people to develop their brains to think in terms of how things work on the battlefield. Which, then, you play the game and it's like...yeah...probably not.  I mean, granted, I'm not a soldier, a general, nor military strategist, so perhaps I'm wrong. But the game fails to capture such issues as terrain, spies, training, and supply networks. Most ancient battles, purportedly, were not set-piece confrontations in an open field, they were sieges to take over centers of power or commerce. Tafl games probably are based on this siege-centric reality to war and so may be a better game if war simulation is the goal.  But more importantly, chess always seems to come down to running through a wide variety of iterations of possible options, to develop an optimal path. A real battle, I sense, is probably more like playing paper-rock-scissors, where you have to guess whether the other guy is going to go left or right, so that you zig or zag appropriately.  Neuroshima Hex is probably (again - not being an expert - but in my opinion) a much better simulation of a battle than chess is. You would have commanders moving people around into different positions, eyeing the other teams troops, to try and spot a moment of advantage and then call in the attack. Once that begins, it will mostly unfold on its own and you're just stuck with the results, hoping that you read everything correctly. Except, of course, in a real battle you would be operating in a fog of war. And chess doesn't even have that.  So...chess is a decent game. But I like to rate things to at least some extent based on what I think the designer was actually trying to achieve. In this case, I feel like it was a bit of a failure, which just happened to be fun despite the fact. ", 6.),
 ('Max Players 5', 5.),

 ("*As indicated by my original review below, Tekhenu really let me down at two players. At the three player count, I was shocked by how much more the core gameplay hook improved.  If Tekhenu wasn't associated with Tascini's pedigree, I would never consider this game for future plays. My initial impression indicated that Tekhenu is nothing more than an overwrought resource management game. Tekhenu's obelisk lighting mechanism is it's primary game hook and unique spin on dice drafting. That obelisk mechanism looked to recapture some of the brilliance and elegance of Tascini's 2012 design Tzolk'in: The Mayan Calendar. Instead Tekhenu's central mechanism requires a great deal of upkeep and contains an annoying puzzle that obfuscates future turn planning. Beyond that, Tekhenu features a fairly standard set of areas/sub mechanisms that all revolve around spending resources. If the foundational dice drafting element of Tekhenu was less cumbersome, I'd likely be more forgiving of the game's secondary aspects. As those secondary aspects currently stand, they are awkwardly complex and exception heavy for what ultimately provide fairly simple results. At the end of our play we felt that selecting a lighter resource management game to play would be preferable to Tekhenu because, it could provide a similar puzzle, would have half the rules, half the upkeep, and play in half the time.  Bryle - 7.5/10 Mario - 7.5/10 Sixtus - 7.5/10", 8.),

 ("This is a pretty light game, as one could expect out of a game that's just dice and cards, but it's not one that has a lot of strategic decision-making driving the game.  You get a hand of cards at the start of the game that give you different combos to complete -- some are as familiar as three-of-a-kind or full houses, while others are a little more unique, and then some even have direct competition between players -- and when you complete the combo, you get a number of chips and discard the card.  The chips go toward helping you accomplish your goals (take an extra roll or an extra turn, give another player a card, or discard one of your cards in hand), so there's some tactics to how you play out your cards, but other than that, it's pretty luck-driven.  There are some cards that only give you one roll to meet the goal, so it's strictly a matter of rolling the right thing, and it's a little frustrating.  It's a short game, though, and I can see that with more players, more choices come into the game, but it's not particularly challenging. In [24]:
results = []
for item in test_records:
    results.append(cl.classify(item[0]))
results_TB = np.array(results)

Looking at the results of this classifier¶

Taking a look at this classifier we can see that the precision has decreased due to the data size being lower. If we take a closer look we can see that the ratios of the difference between the predicted values and actual values have decreased though showing a slight increase in "accruacy" rather than precision.

In [25]:
print(findAccuracy(y_test_TB, results))

Mixing the findings from Vader, TextBlob, and vanilla Sci-kit¶

After testing out each method used here. I found that Sci-kit's resources are incredibly fast and optimized, so In practice I'd like to use an approach with it. Additionally we found that smoothing these didn't have too much of an effect on the overall data.
From Vader, I found that it actually might be best to just transition to losing the sentiment values from training and just use each word as a set of features for our training, as seen in TextBlob due to the increase of accruacy in results. However, TextBlob's problem with processing large amounts of data comes from its feature extraction method. So to speed this up we'll use essentially the same method it uses but port it to sci-kit.
Here we break our data as usual into train and test. Then create a pipeline with a vectorized feature extractor for each review, then train it on the NaiveBayes classifier.

In [28]:
X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(df[['comment','rating']],df['rating'], test_size=0.2)

Taking a look at the results¶

We can see that the precision and accruacy has increased. Creating a smaller grouping closer to zero.

In [29]:
model = make_pipeline(TfidfVectorizer(), MultinomialNB())
%time model.fit(X_train['comment'], X_train['rating'])
%time results_model = model.predict(X_test['comment'])
print(findAccuracy(y_test, results_model))
